RIngling . Brothers' circus. The Grand
Jury indicted him for murder. His attor
ney? made a motion to quash the Indict

dictment. - The motion was denied.

As Is the case with the British defenses
at Esquimau, this conning tower will bo

TACOMA. Oct. 28.â Government experts
are superintending the construction of a
conning tower on one of the commanding 1
eminences of Fort Worden, on Point Wil
son, near Port Townsend, overlooking a
long stretch of the Straits of Juan de
Fuca. The tower is provided with a sys
tem of mirrors which reflect on a pre
pared chart the exact position of any ves
sel entering the straits. These waters are
divided on the charts into small squares,
the necessary elevation for the gunworlc
on any square being prepared in advance.
In the event of invasion the observer sim
ply communicates with the "man behind
the guns" the information that the inva
der is in a certain square. From this "in
formation the bombardment is at once
commenced* with great prospects for sink
ing the incoming vessel.

SANTA ANA. Oct. 28.â After raining in

Crops, especially celery, have befen great

The rain will be of great benefit to the

pears. Thirty-two carloads were shipped

With the opening of the new year will
be organized one of the greatest steel
corporations in the world.. Capitalists , in
this city and throughout the Mahoning
Valley will be largely interested as stock
holders in the new corporation.. The men
who were largely responsible for the
great success of the underlying steel
companies constituting the United States
Steel Corporation, and who disposed of
their holdings, will be prominently identi
fied with the new company. Several In
dependent steel companies which have re
fused {to join the United States Steel
Corporation will also come in.
H. C. Frick of Pittsburg will, it is said,
be president of the new corporation, and
the capital stock will be 12,000,000,000. Ne
gotiations are' now in progress with
Pittsburg. Youngstown and Philadelphia
capitalists with a view of bringing the
interested parties together in a solid J
organization. Little ' information will be\
given until- the charter for the corporaâ w.

CALL BUREAU. 1406 G STREET. N.
W., "WASHINGTON, Oct. 28.â What will
be probably the most comprehensive pro
gramme ever submitted by the board of
naval officers to the Navy Department
for transmission' to Congress will be sub
mitted this week to Secretary Long of
the Board on Construction. The pro
gramme i urged to-day by the . members of
the board for adoption included: Four
first class battleships of 15,000 tons dis
placement," carrying the heaviest- arma
ment and protected by the thickest armor
possible on such displacement; four arm
ored cruisers of 14,000 tons displacement,
of high speed, carrying the heaviest arm
ament and the thickest armor compati
ble with displacement and speed; eight
een gunboats for. service in South Amer
ica and the Philippines of more than 1200
tons displacement; twelve gunboats of a
few hundred tons displacement each, for
service In the Philippines. In addition, it
is the purpose of the board to recommend
the construction of auxiliary ships, in
cluding colliers, training ships and an en
gineering repairing vessel. No torpedo
boats or submarine-boats have been sug
gested for incorporation in the pro
gramme, and it is-extremely doubtful if
any construction of these types will.be
considered. It is the intention of the;
board to hold another meeting on Wed
nesday, when there will be a renewal, of
the discussion relative to the character
istics of the programme to be submitted.
No naval officer has lost sight of the
action taken by Germany to expedite the
completion of its programme of ship con
struction, and there is reason to belleva
that this matter will enter into the con
sideration of the Board on Construction.
' In' conversation to-day with Represen
tative Fitzgerald, who represents the
Brooklyn Navy Yard district, and Repre
sentative Rupert of New York City, Pres
ident Roosevelt declined to make any rec
ommendations for the construction of tha
warships in navy yards as suggested by
the Brooklyn Congressman, on the ground
that such recommendation should prop
erly come through the Navy Department.
He said the subject had been discussed
with the Secretary of the Navy on sev
eral previous occasions and the latter has
invariably held that the construction of
new warships can be accomplished cheap
er and to better advantage to the Govern
ment at private shipyards. *>.:..> â¢
Some of the members of the board thin*
the programme considered to-day too ex
tensive, insisting that the shipbuilding ca
pacity of the country cannot accommo
date so many new vessels. Other mem
bers hold that as Congress adopted no
authorization of additional anmorclads at
the last session of Congress it is desira
ble to ask that a programme be adopted
which shall make provision for the ships
that should have been laid down during
the present year, as well \u25a0 as those which
in the usual course" would be provided for
in the law to be passed during the com
ing session.

PYDTG HIGHWAYttAN.
T2XIiS OF HIS CRIMES
WASHINGTON, Oct. 2S.â Only one de
cision \ras rendered in the United States
Supreme Court to-day. It was the case of
Kred A. McMasters, administrator of F.
E. HcMasters, formerly a resident of the
State of Iowa. The case involved the
<jues;tions as to whether an insurance pol
icy soes into effect on the date of the ap
plication for it or when the policy is de
livered. In this instance the application
xras made on December 12, 1893, and' tha
policy, was delivered December 26. 1893,
when the premium was paid. Provision
â¢was made for grace of a month in the
matter of payment of the second policy.
McMasters died January 18, 1E95. six days
after the expiration of the- month of
grace, if it was to be computed from De
cember 12. or eigrht days before its ex
piration, computing it from the 26th of
December, the anniversary of the delivery
cf the premium. The Supreme Court de
cided that the grace began only after the
delivery of the premium, thus holding' the
policy to be good. The opinion of the Cir
cuit Court ol Appeals for the Eighth Cir
cuit was reversed.

As a large portion of the army is stA
tioned in the western part of this United
States and over half west of the Pacific
Ocean. General Miles recommends the es
tablishment of a military post in South
ern California. He also recommends the
establishment of a war college in the city
of Washington.
General Miles refers to a number cf"
recommendat'ons which he made in for
mer reports looking to the improvement
of the condition of the army; also to or
ders he has issued during the year to ac
complish this purpose, i He speaks espe
cially of the experienc*, intelligence and
efficiency of the troops of the Unite!
States and the success tney have had '.n
China and the Philippines, and says they
have borne the rigors of the Arctic cli
mate in Alaska and the heat of the
tropics, and under all circumstances
maintained the character of the Ameri
can army. He recommends that the mili
tary posts throughout the country be put
in excellent shape for troops that are re-Â»
turned from service beyond the seas.
While Congress has made ample provision for
the management of military affairs In the or
ganization of the army, whereby the companies,
regiments, brigade*, divisions and departments
are made the units of administration and by
statute has clothed the officials not only -with
executive authority but with judicial powers
and responsibility, yet the tendency has been
to absorb and usurp the entire conduct of the
military establishment in the city of Washing
ton, and especially in the staE departments.
This has been found most injurious in other
armies and is one of the principal defects in
our own system. The evil has been Increasing
during the activities of the wars of the last
three years to an extent that, in my Judgment,
requires serious consideration, and I recommend
that decentralization be effected as far as pob
eible and that all proper and lawful authority
be restored to eubordinate commanders who are
provided with an efficient organization and who
\u25a0 can be safely Intrusted with responsibility for
the efficient and faithful administration of
military affairs commensurate with their im
portant commands.
General Miles does not approve of the
present organization of the artillery
corps, saying that it establishes another
bureau in Washington. He believes in
the former regimental organizations*.
Speaking of the army canteen, which is
abolshed by the army reorganization law,
he says that no injury has resulted, and
in the main the law has been beneficial.
General Miles states his objections to
the management of military affairs in the
War Department in the following lan
guage :

Did they fail to take advantage of it as an

it was proposed to buy Mission Rock, a deso
late rock about a mile from the shore â¢ of
Butchertown, which was worth at most about
$500. It is a rock about fifty feet square, stand
ing alone, open to the winds, in the middle of
the bay. They proposed to pay for that rock,
worth $500, the sum of {15,000. Where was the
$14,500 to go, ladies and gentlemen? It was to
go Into the- same capacious maw that has swal
lowed millions of the dollars raised in 'this city
and county by taxation, and the many, many
millions of dollars raised besides . by: the cor
rupt bribery of corporations. We came .into of
flce, gentlemen, when that scheme was almost
carried out, but at the last moment it was ex
posed. It was exposed in the press, there was
an outcry on. the part of the Merchants' Asso
ciation, on the part of the capitalists interested,
ana the thing was stopped. We came into of
fice and the same subject was raised. How did
we provide for it? The Merchants' Association
proposed to us a site which they said could be
purchased for $15,000. We chose three reputable
real estate agents to appraise that site. They
appraised it at $15,000. We were not satisfied.
We made a provision in the budget for the pur-,
chase of that site for $12,000. and last week the
sale was about completed at that figure.

Mr. Chairman, ladies and: gentlemen: I am
not able to appear to you In very stirring words
this evening. I have traveled over the entire
city to-day, and In addition to the physical ex
ertion I lost the few notes that I had prepared
on my way here.
I am told that in an afternoon paper, which I
never read â not that the reading of it would
disturb my equanimity of temper, but because
it does not print any news (laughter)â I am
told that this afternoon paper reprinted a story
from the Examinerâ to which the Examiner,
by the way. was fair enough to append my de
nial â I am told that this statement is repeated
in an afternoon paper, the reputation of -which
is entlrely(?) established, and the proprietor,
whose character for honesty, integrity and im
peccability la so well established it would do
me no harm to deny. Conscious as I am. ladies
and gentlemen, of all these things, I tUink it
will do me no harm to deny absolutely, wholly
and completely the entire story.
It is false in whole, it is false in part. The
very fact that I voted for the passage of thfs
bill, the very fact that it was passed by the
entire Board of Supervisors without a dissent
ing voice, is answer enough to this. This is
the statement of a thing without honor, a thing
that creeps, a thing without honesty. Gen
tlemen, this is the statement of a thins that
has grown fat and loathsome upon blackmail.
Gentlemen. I cannot soil my hands nor my
tongue further to-night. . I shall allude no
further to such people. I hold them in the con
tempt which their base character merits;.
We say," have we done well or ill' by the citi
zens? If we have done well. - then you - must
support us. -whether you are Republicans or
whether you are Democrats. If we^have no'.
then you must discharge us. no matter what
our party affiliations maybe. â¢*..* ".

LABOR CANDIDATES SPEAK.
tiD has been given out from
II the headquarters of the allied cor-
I porations that everything can be
UL thrown overboard as a last resort
to save Wells from defeat. All
the other candidates on his ticket are to
be Facrfflced to pull Wells through. The
trading and the knifing cause indignation
among Republican nominees, and every
man is bent on saving himself. The utter
defeat of Wells seems inevitable. . The
poor impression that he made in the be
ginning of the campaign in saying that
Mr. Herrin agreed with him that B. P.
Flint should go on the ticket has not been
removed by his subsequent utterances.
Voters who heard his speech at Garibaldi
Hall last week thought it was the worst
campaign speech ever made until they
heard his talk at B'nai B'rith Hall last
night. Every time that the corporation
nominee for Mayor makes any attempt
to discuss living questions he displays a
lamentable ignorance of the public af
fairs. If his managers would prepare and
typewrite something for him -and charge
him particularly to read it and not rely
on his memory to repeat it, he would
make a much better impression than he
has yet made. The worst feature of the
whole affair from one point of view is that
Sam Shortridge. who is out getting a rep
utation for ora-tory, is using poor Wells
as a foil. Sam shows off to tine advan
tage as a speaker in comparison with
Wells.
Bosses Imploring' Lieutenants.
The bosses who put Wells at the head
of the ticket are sending out for the dis
trict leaders who are not in line and im
ploring them to get in for Wells.
Promises of support in the next cam
paign are fr?ely given. The balky leaders
are not asked to support the Supervisors
or candidates other than Mayor, but just
to get in and save the head of the ticket
from ruin. Everything now points to the
defeat of Wells. Republican merchants,
property owners and taxpayers generally
have resolved to support Tobin. In the
Twenty-eighth, Twenty-ninth, Forty
fourth and Forty-fifth districts there is a
strong Republican tendency toward the
support of Schmitz. Politicians who have
studied the situation estimate that Wells
will not get one-half the Republican vote
of the city. Two weeks ago the betting
was even money for Wells against the
field. There has been a complete turn
about since. The only Wells money
Offered is on a proposition of Tobin
against the field.
Promises of Places.
The recent order of the bosses to cut
everybody to save Wells Is inciting a
spirit of hostility toward the head of
the boss ticket. From now on Herrin and
Burns will draw freely on the brick
yard. Everything in the way of patronage
at the City Hall will be plastered with
ten layers of promises in order to catch
votes for the head of the ticket. It is
quite evident that the "boys" who want
recognition will not be fooled again. >Iany
of the promises made in the Senatorial
canvass and In the campaign last fall
have not been kept. Sam Rainey Is do
ing his hardest work to save Wells from
defeat* but his followers are cutting loose
to save something for themselves.

Alviso will probably experience a boom,
for within a month a line of automobiles
will be running between that place and
San Jose, and another electric road, to
be built by San Jose capital, is among
the possibilities.
A 1 fast steamer service between Al
viso and San Francisco will connect with
the new road, and that it -will be an ac
tive competitor with the Southern Pacific
for freight and passengers is assured.
' The' purchasers were here a few weeks
ago looking over the ground, but they
left without disclosing their plans. A
lme terminal site of sixty acres goes with
tha property. It is right on tidewater
at Alvlso, and now that the government
has just completed dredging the slough
the property is valuable. The right. of
way is sixty feet -wide, and extends a
distance of eight miles. The old plan of
the San Jose and^Alviso Railroad in
tended the terminal to be near the Hotel
Vendome. and It Is believed this new cor
poration, has made arrangements for sta
tion facilities in about the same place.
Only light grading will have to be done,
and it would take but a few months'
work to put the line into operation..
Just who is back of the project ia
not known, and one rumor has it that the
road will be used for the Santa Fe,
which will make boat connection at Al
viso. Work 13 to be rushed, and, it Is
said, cars will be running in a few
months.
I SAN JOSE, Oct. 28.â San Jose and Al
viso are to be qonnected by an electric
railway, which will handle freight and
passengers. Eastern capitalists are in
terested In the project, and they, have
purchased the right of way obtained a
few years ago by the San Jose and Al
viso Railroad Company for a steam rail
road. . . . * . . ' â¢ -

Communication with Asia is most important
to u s at present because our trade will be with
Asia, not with Europe. The Islands as coal-
Ing stations and bases of naval supplies are
of infinite importance for protection of our line
of communication.
Communication â that is. forming lines of
commerce â affects greatly national expansion.
The acquisition of Porto Rico, Hawaii and the
Philippines is Important in that these points
ere necessary in completing our lines of com
munication. The United States has been par
ticu:ar!y fortunate in stumbling upon such pos
sefs.ons as she has acquired in the Philippines
and Hawaii.
What are the motives operative in bringing
al>out the development from the national state
to national imperialism? Colonization has been
dÂ«=flnÂ»d as the exertion of Influence by a. more
hip-hly civilized state over a less highly civil
ized state, with the purpose of elevating the
latter. Migration of Chinese to the United
States is not colonization, therefore, nor is the
migration of an entire nation to a new demaln.
The motives or causes In the development of
national imperlali-m are: Overpopulation, mis
sionaries, the adventuresome spirit of Individ
uals, commerce, communication, industrial de
velopment, direct occupation of territory with
the idea of enlarging the national power. Over
population is seldom a cause of colonizing at
lirft but It keeps ur> colonies once organized.
Missionaries are of far greater Importance. In
ca.Â«e of nations having low forms of religion
missionaries can often have direct influence
on :;he government. In countries like China
and India, however, their Influence Is more
indirect because of the large number of persons
to influence and the strong pre-established re
ligicn. The murder of missionaries has often
led to the conquest of territory.
Individual adventure is a most important mo
tive in the expansion of European interests.
Commercial expansion Is another important
xnot.ve. Present world politics is to-Â«. certain
extent a struggle for markets. All nations
begi.n making the commerce of their colonies
the exclusive possession of the mother country.
Wits the growth of Liberalism these restric
tions fell and by 1S60 the policy of free trade
\u25a0was In full sway. Recently, however, the ar-
KUir,ent that "commerce follows the flag" has
beer. made, reviving the old idea. Statistics
Fhow that the greater commerce is between
rival nations, consequently it would be utter
felly to chop off the trade of other nations In
order to secure the colonial trade.
ThÂ« idea that there will be a world unity Is
looking coo far Into the future to be politically
practical. National imperialism is a new de
velopment, but it does not lead of necessity
to a world-state. Roman imperialism had as
Its core a city-state and as its aim the con
<iues: of other cities. The core of our present
cyetrm is the national state. National imper
ialism recognizes a number of national sys
tems.
J'hÂ«ire may be said to be five stages in the
evolution of political society. First, the agrl
cuUurai etaee. where the clan was paramount;
tecord, the stage of caste or feudal society;
third, the city stage, as first developed in
Greece, where aristocratic caste privileges were
discarded and all freemen were equal, with
the rower not extending beyond the city's lim
its; fourth, the territorial stage, where the
cities or States were united under -political
leadersâ princes and kingsâ the unity being ter
ritorial and artificial; fifth, the national stage,
in wlaich the world powers are to-day.
The English Llberalists believe in the fourth
Ftagt. Individualism without governmental in
terference is the basis of Liberalismâ hence the
doctrine of free trade. We are gradually
throwing over the Liberal idea that govern
ment is a mere mechanism and are recogniz
ing i.he fact that we are integral parts of the
gove-nment.
STANFORD UNIVERSITY. Oct. 28.-
Dr. Paul .S. Reinsch. professor of political
science in the University of Wisconsin,
delivered to-day the first of a series of
four lectures on "World Politics" to be
given before the students of Stanford
University, taking- for his subject "The
New Imperialism." Dr. Reinsch said In
part;
Uurlng the two decades from 1850 to 1S70
hardly a statesman In England, Prance or
Geriracy was enthusiastic on the subject of
colonies. At this time Gladstone and other
English statesmen looked forward to the ulti
mate separation of the British colonies from
the raothcr country and they believed such ac
tion advisable. John Bright, In speaking of
Canada, said that It would be allowed to choose
Us courseâ either to -separate from or remain
with the mother country. The statesmen of to
day a far different view.

DENVER, Oct. 28.â President John
W. Springer of the National Livestock
Association to-day made public a unique
plan, of Western stockmen to attract the
interest if not the co-operation of Presi
dent Roosevelt in legislation which they
will ask of Congress at its approaching
session. This is no less than a parade. of
"cow. punchers',' through the streets 'of
Washington and up to the door of the
White House.
' The annual convention of the Live
stock Association meets in Chicago De
cember 3. . Immediately after its adjourn*
ment the delegates from Colorado, Wyo
ming, Texas, New Mexico, Arizona and
other Western States will take a special
train for Washington. They will go in
the costume of the range and will have
a car or two of "bucking" broncos along
and a supply of cowboys to ride them.
When they arrive in the - Capital City
they will 'form in line. and parade, and
with the cowboy band from Pueblo lead-
Ing. them will march to the White House
for a call on the President. Springer will
be loaded with a speech on the . need of
legislation on land leasing, . the \u25a0 Federal
inspection of woolen goods and shoddy
and the oleomargarine business, which he
will deliver to the President. It is be-,
lieved that *\u25a0 the novel method of \u25a0 calling
on the President will have a: good effect
and that he may be prevailed on to help
the cattle men secure their demands

SANTA ROSA, Oct. 28.â J. Lev! Likens
of Healdsburg, charged with responsibil
ity for the death of Warren Rogers, with
whom he had a flght a short time ago, has
been discharged from custody. At the
preliminary examination the evidence did
not show anything that tended to connect
him with the death of Rogers. In his ante
mortem statement Rogers charged Likens
with being responsible for his condition.
The postmortem examination revealed
the fact that- death was due to gangrene
of the lungs, caused by pneumonia.

REDWOOD CITY, Oct. 28.â A second
will of the late William H. Howard was
filed here for probate to-day by the
widow, who asks that she be appointed
executrix thereof. This will was exe
cuted in 1887, while " thÂ© first one filed
bears date eleven years later. . .
By the terms of the will last In data
most of the property of deceased goes to
the children, five in number. Mrs. Anna
D Howard, the widow, gets but a one
sixth interest in certain personal prop
erty. \u25a0 Under the will filed to-day the
homestead property at San Mateo, com
prising' 350 acres of land, with the Im
provements thereon, and all the furni
ture, carriages, Works of art. silver,
books and personal effects, are given to
her absolutely, and all the residue of the
property, real and personal. Is given to
her in trust, the income to be applied to
her own support and the education and
maintenance of her children.
A lively-legal battle ia expected when
the case comes up for hearing.
Superintendent Davies had long known
that the Carbon Hill coal was good for
coking purposes, and he therefore recom
mended that coke ovens be built. After
due investigation this 'course wa3 deter
mined upon. The output will be shipped
to the' smelters In Tacoma, Everett, Mon

TACOMA, Oct. 28.â Seventy large mod
ern coke^ovensjare being built at Carbon
ado by the .Southern Pacific Railway;
which owns the Carbon Hill coal mines,
having a dally capacity of more than 1000
tons. This enterprise is one of the direct
results of the introduction of oil as fuel
on the locomotives of the South
ern Pacific at Oakland and San
Francisco. For many years every pound
of coal which the Carbon Hill mines could
supply has been shipped by fast steam
colliers to those cities. The demand was
lessened, commencing-"* about a year ago,
and for a time it was intended to restrict
the output at Carbonado.

REDDING, Oct. 28.â Pathetic is the
case of .John N. Gatherne, a youris Eng
lishman of good - family, who came to
Redding for his health some time ago.
His mind failed and he was committed to
Napa Asylum. His wealthy parents have
hurried over from England to his succor,
only to be confronted at the asylum by
the refusal of the authorities to allow
them to take their boy home with them.
Gatherne came to Redding more than
a year ago. He had been in delicate
health at the country seat of his parents
in England, and it was hoped the change
would benefit him. It Is now said he hai
suffered some mental trouble. A friend
had assured Gatherne in London of the
wlldness of the country and its necessi
ties, and he arrived with seven trunks full
of outlandish cowboy outfits, small arms
and ammunition. He joined the English
colony, rode the range for his health, be
came popular socially and ranked as one
of the best tennis players of the county.
A month ago Gatherne became pos
sessed of the hallucination that he was
being pursued by murderers. He grew
violent and was put in a padded cell. In
a week he was liberated, with a guard,
and his condition was cabled to his par
ents. No answer came back. Gatherne
grew violent again and was committed
to Napa Insane Asylum by Judge Edward
Sweeney.
It seems that J. Gatherne and his wif?
sailed immediately from London upon
learning of their son's dementia. Tney
wired ahead * while coming, from New
York, and learning that he was in Napa.
went thither. The officials, when aske-J
to allow them to take their son, who is
no longer violent, back to England, sai3
they had no power to do so after his com
mitment. The' distracted parents came
here and learned the full circumstances
and to-day went back to Napa. They
will take the case into court, if possible,
in an attempt to force the delivery of
their unfortunate son to them.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
The last vestige of the smallpox scare
disappeared a week ago, | when permis
sion was granted the Indians to go on the
dock when steamers were in port. This
had not been allowed for more than six
months for fear of contagion.
Homer Bird, who has once been sen
tenced to death for murder in Alaska, is
again on trial for his life in Juneau.
About six years ago Bird, in company
with three male .comrades and one fe
male, were gold prospecting on the Yu
kon. They were living on a small boat.
According to the testimony introduced,
Bird shot two of his companions without
cause and drove another ashore. The
woman escaped. Bird was caught through
the' efforts of the man who was driven
ashore. He was tried, convicted adn
sentenced to be hanged, but was granted
a new trial through technicalities.
Work on the immense Government coal
shed at Japonsky Island was stopped for
a ' few hours this week by reason of a
strike. There has long been bitterness
between the men and Foreman Young. A
new foreman was appointed .â¢ and peace
was restored.
SITKA, Alaska, Oct. 19 (via Seattle,
Oct. 28).â According. to reliable informa
tion recently received here, the. Rodman
Bay, mines are likely to exceed in magni
tude the famous Treadwell mines on
Douglass Island. Rodman Bay is about
sixty miles from Sitka and the mines are
located on Baranoff Island, in a valley
eight miles from tidewater. The mines
are' operated by the Rodman Bay Com
pany, a corporation financed principally
with English capital.
The ore tested shows itself to be of a
low grade, but in -great, quantity. It. is
\u25a0believed that the mountain, at the foot of
which the shafts will be sunk, is a solid
mass of low-grade ore, running from $3
to S4 to the. ton.
"Work, on all the mines in the vicinity
of Silver Bay, seven miles from "Sitka,
has been pushed to the utmost this sum
mer. \u25a0 -

ATTORNEYS APPEAL TO THE COURT
AND COMMISSIONER GETS A REST
The taking of testimony in the Nome contempt cases before Commissioner Hcacock was
interrupted yesterday by arguments made on motion before the Circuit Court of Appeals. The Gov
ernment' has finished its cast;, with the exception of placing two. other zvitnesseson the stand, men
who have not yet arrived from Nome. To-day the attorneys representing the respondent, Arthur
H. Noyes/ will appear before Judges Ross, Morrow and Gilbert, sitting as the Circuit Court of
Appeals, and ask* for a continuance until such time as the court may designate just what portion
of the testimony already' taken shall be met by them. They allege that the testimony so far pro
duced is largely hearsay, opinion and deduction. Their arguments will rest on these allegations.

A CHANCE TO SMILE.
Only one public man-in the South has denounced
snd deprecated the wcrk of Washington among the
'members of his race, and that is the inexpressible
Tillman of South Carolina, who declares that the in
dustrial education of the negro is a detriment to the
country! We do not believe that the Maryland
voters wiil be influenced by caricatures of the Presi
'dent or his wife, but if they. can be this ie a good time
! to find it. out.
The North has not been harsh nor hasty with. the
South in its struggle with the race problem. ,Jf that
struggle is for the preservation of society there is no
to interfered. But if.it is a manifestation
of mere prejudice, an .outbreak of personal ' hatred
and a sodden and sordid expression of enmity tcrthe
negro for. wjiat he can't help, his color" and his racial
character, the views of the -North are subject to
change and the power of the constitution may be in
voked to protect thexWack 'man, who, left to himself,
ctn^proteet his- rights only, by the miracle of changing
his skin. To reasonable men it appears that when a
negro, bd.rn.in slavery, raises .himself by his own ex
ertions and genius as Booker Washington has* done,'
U'is.the first duty of, good citizenship to. recognize
.and encourage him.: : It goes- without saying that if
all, or a majority, or a large fraction, of the negroes
' in this country were, equal. to.: h'im, it would be better
for the country and a glad thing for both races. -
Therefore, by encouraging him t let others.be en
couraged to be like him. : . President Roosevelt .^ has
.acted from a profound philosophy and in line ', with
the peace and 'welfare of his country by- giving' this
foremost negro in the world the recognition mer
ited by his talents, training and work.
*.'. Those who turn it into an appeal to prejudice and
, ignorance arÂ« not acting" from philosophy nor patriot
} ism. Their motive is the ; s6rdid J bne of office-getting.
Professor Washington has hot regretted the wresting
of the ballot and .of political power from the South
ern negro,, for he sees 'no good to him in the posses
sion of -a\power that he does -not know how to use.
His object is to enlarge the: negro's means of liveli
. hood by manual training, .increasing his knowledge
snd-his-skili in the economic use of his capacity. -He
wants him educated to want a better house,, to .be bet
ter clad, to own real property and to be trained to get
all these by his own exertions. One may well pity
a man who has no sympathy for such a work and no
encouragement for the earnest man who has started
and prosecuted it s'o promisingly among the "people
.of his race. "â¢ "\u25a0\u25a0 ' : . - \./' ' ;'C-- '\u25a0\u25a0i." v -\ â¢ \ .

ATTACKING THE PRESIDENT.
CZARâ E. G., City. Czar is the English
designation of the Emperor of, Russia.
The Russian Is Tzare, with the e mute.
It is a corruption of the Latin Caesar.
The Russians also use Tsar. In that lan
guage Czarttza and Tsaritsa are the
equivalent of Czarina. Tzar, sometimes
Tsar, is the form*, while the Russian la
Tsare. One etymologist asserts that Czar
Is derived from the old Slavonic language
and signifies very much the same as the
German Kaiser and the Latin Caesar, to
which it probably owes Its origin. Somo
etymologists identify it with the termina
tion of the names of the old Assyrian
kings, such as Phalassar, Nabonassar and
Nabopolassar. The title of Tsare was first
given to the Grand Duke Vladimir, who
reigned 950 to 1015.
Never having tried either of the methods
this department cannot say anything as
to their efficacy. Â»
TO REMOVE VARNISH-E., City. The
following are given as methods to remove
varnish from an oil painting: "Remove
the picture from the 'frame. "Wash tho
painting with a weak solution of carbo
nate of ammonia, wiping it off with a
sponge wetted with water as soon as it
has fulfilled its purpose. If allowed to
remain too long it will Injure: the oil col
ors. Another way is to spread a thick
coat of wet fuller's earth over the surface
of the varnish, leaving it on long enough
to soften it. It may then be removed by
washing."
The "Ligue Contre le Mai de Mer" may
/never be able to discover a way of totally
preventing seasiekness, but it may be
able to so direct and instruct people in
hygienic methods that an ocean voyage
will cease to be-the martyrdom that it
: now is for those who are "poor sailors "
For example, one will know whether or
not it is advisable to eat heartily before
embarking, and what kind of food must
be taken. It is evident that if among a
thousand persons who have eaten before
embarking there is found, to be^a greater
number of cases of seasickness than
among the same number of .persons who
have not eaten, the main part of the ques
tion will have already, been answered.
As soon as the Ligue has a sufficient
number of adherents to constitute a
strong "moral force" It " will endeavor to
bring to bear a pressure that will oblige
the steamship companies to follow the
elementary. rules of hygiene in the mat
ter of table fare. It will also give its
support to the inventors of suspension ap
paratus .that are intended to counteract
the motion of 'vessels.
a general correspondence on thfs vital
topic, and letters are coming in by thou
sands. From the tone of the majority "of
these letters it will be possible to ascer
tain under what conditions seasiskness is
developed In the majority of persons, and
consequently a definite problem can be
proposed for solution.
PERSONAL MENTION.
I" T is Impossible that all - the persons
â¢ who have ever suffered from seasick
ness have heard of the.Ligue Contre
le'Mal de Mer. It is also improbable
that' those who have "paid their trib
ute" from the ship's deck would hesitate
very long about paying a visit to the
headquarters of this well meaning Ligue
if they were reasonably certain of learn
ing how the terrors of seasickness could
be averted. . ....
The object of the "League Against Sea
sickness" is to bring together all -those
who are "interested" in the' subject of
seasickness as a result ; of having had
practical experience in that line and who
want to be rendered immune fo'r all time.
The Matin remarks that the Frenchman
who has once had' seasickness will never
travel on the perfidious element again as
long as he can travel on land, and that
this explains the lack of colonial develop
ment on the. part of the French and some
of the difficulties experienced by that na
tion In Its relation with other countries.
The Ligue Contre le Mai de Mer is hope
ful of .being able, to do something practi
cal with this malady, which is so regu
larly made a subject of ridicule. on land,
but which is sometimes such a serious
matter on board ship' that the victims
would' like^ to 'be thrown Into the -sea.
The secretary of l the Ligue \u25a0 has opened
Association Founded ta Ascertain Under. What Conditions
Malady Develops and to Seek Remedies. ...

England in 1S04 and was first cultivated

FOUR years have passed since S. A.
Andree started for; the North Pole;
in a "balloon. ' . The Swedish courts
, have declared that the explorer
may legally be supposed to be dead.
Andree's; own extreme limit ot time has
passed, geographers agree that he is lost,
his own brother has accepted the bequests
made to him in Andree's will; yet his
mother ; and his sister are still confident
that he is alive and will return. -
-These two .women * live at Grenna in
Sweden. They keep themselves ready to
receive him any day or night and dust and
clear every, morning the rooms in which
he made the plans for his expeditions. Say
what you may, produce .the ':-\u25a0. most ' con
vincing proof that Andree must be dead;
bring forth; the ,'strongest. arguments "in
favor of. your contention, the mother and
sister will listen quietly 'and when you
have finished will simply say: "Hah'lefver
och will, otervanda." \\ (He : is -alive and
will return.) . . ( \u25a0-!
'It .is not- merely hope, not* merely â¢ a"
strong desire.fthat makes these women so
absolutely certain of the' return of their
son and brothei*. It is their belief thfit
Andree went forth to seek the North. Pole
and : to.find it "in fulfillment of a mission
given to him by God. "And the Lord has
never- forsaken- one -of -his servant9i"-.is
the way they explain it. { ; v >j !
.The mother, Fru Minna Andree, is near-;
iy.70^ though* her clear, ruddy face,.- her
erect, carriage.. arid the continuous smile
f hat -jP!ay s around her,, face make her-ap

daughter, Fru Emeline Spanberg, is^a
lively', gbcd-natured' woman, and" mother
and;.daughter impresV the" visitor as. a
pair of .the'/nios't intimate" friends. The
.â.->*- . . .; . .'.-. .'. . -\u25a0.....' .\u25a0"'. '" .-._"â¢-'\u25a0\u25a0_ i
cottage they live in is a roomy, one-story
building, furnished simply. Most of the
furniture is substantial oak and an" etch
ing or two relieves the somberness of the
walls. y .-.
They are devout .worndft and Fru Andree
invokes grace before each meal, "And,
O Lord, kee# thy servant, my son, and
return. him safely," she has .ended her
prayer every day for these four â¢ years.
"Amen! Amen!" responds Fru Spanberff.
It is impossible to describe the glow of
pride and satisfaction which spreads over
the kindly face of the mother when she
exhibits the' first shoe her son wore, when
she .points out the first, prayer book. he
used, when she holds up to your inspection
the white, tie he wore at his confirmation.
And when she opens the drawer and takes
out the various parchments which testify
to the -success her son in high school
and, university, "-when she handles the va
rious medals he received, when she^asks
you to. read tne'flattering letters in which
countless scientific societies notified him
of his. electionjas one of their members,
then, indeed, you see. in her motherhood
glorified. ... . ._ ._..',...
"Has there ever., been a mother more
blessed- than' 1?" ; she .asks..- and it must
besaid- that- her face gives, the answer.
"From â¢boyhood, on my. boy has. felt that,
like the apostles of old, he must go to
parts unknown and preach the gospel.
Not in the same way as- the regular mis
sionaries. Oh, no! -He \u25a0 was never much
of a talker. \u25a0 But God gave him the ca
pacity and opportunity to' study and that
is also one way to -make God's goodness
known. -Yes, he was .to find the North
Pole, and by. his discovery of this much
sought-after region to testify before the
world of the' greatness of our Lord."

THE ambition, of San- Francisco is to .have a strong/ man for. .Mayor. A man is
needed who has , no strings on him, but has the independence to do justice* to all
men and to: every interest.^ Look ;\vhere you will tat the history municipal
misg-overnmeht in this country and its odium begins *' in making national party r
divisions the main issue. in the election of a city government. â¢ â¢ â¢
': \u25a0 t It is a fixed habit of the American people to vote with party in every matter that
concerns -national politics. When a, Senator is to be elected '.; gobdrmqn habitually,/ and
open-eyed, vote for bad candidates bf their party for the Legislature lest a seat in . the
Senate should be lost. The misfortune in city government : begins: in > making partisans
believe that they owe this same allegiance in'a city election and :to a .municipal ticket.
â¢iThat belief has no foundation. Such allegiance is no more due in a'city election than it
is .in the transaction of business. "No man employs a Democratic lawyer on account, of
_his_politics. No man trusts his money with a bank because its officers are of his party.
fin, the employment of men whose work is necessary in business, ho inquiry is made into
\u25a0their politics. Partisanship ceases in all business matters, and running the government

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand- column- and the - successive - tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time; the
fourth time column gives the last tide of the
day, except -when there are but three tides, as
sometimes occurs. The heights given are In ad
dition to the soundings of the United States
Coast Survey Charts, except when a minus (â )
sign precedes the height.: and then the number
given fa subtracted from the depth given by the
charts. The plane of reference Is the mean of
the lower low waters. . .
% Beautiful Crystallized Minerals. S
The collection contains many rare spe
cimens , not. generally found in a private
cabinet and embraces besides rich ores and
ornamental stones hundreds of attractive
and beautiful crystallized minerals from
different \ parts . of America and foreign
lands.
; San Francisco on receiving this gener
ous gift can boast of having the finest
collection of geodes on the Pacific Coast,
if not in the country. There are about
thirty specimens of these and no two
alike.
The collection is very rich in calcltes
of various forms and colors and fluorites
in groups of beautiful crystals in differ
ent shades of red. pink, purple, gTeen
yellow, white, etc.,' from different parts
of the world, the celebrated English fluors
being fully represented.
The group of quartz Is particularly in
teresting, there being many
crystallized specimens of various forms,
including quartz in phantom crystalsâ
these freaks of nature which show I a â¢
crystal within a crystal. There are fine
specimens of amethyst in crystallized and
massive form from Mexico and foreign
countries and beautiful hematites.
There is a splendid group of silver min
erals, n,ot merely ore-bearing rock, but at
tractive specimens rich in free wire silver.
. â¢ Other Rare Specimens.
Other specimens are the rare mineral
known to science as arguerite, a combi
nation of silver and quicksilver, found in
its natural state, and the very rare min
erals selenium, osmium, iridium-and plat
inum.
: Other specimens .that are most inter
esting are a mineral curio which resem
bles a fungous growth on a decaying tree,
known to the mineral world as bernardi
nite; a specimen which looks like a piece
of white leather is known to scientists
under the' name of .plllnite;' a "crystal of
halite (salt) contains several water bub
blesrthe water being Inclosed In cavities,
which can be seen to move as the speci
men Is shaken or vibrated.
â¢The collection numbers about 800 speci
mens and is valued by its former owner
at $10,000. It is deemed one of the finest
private cabinets in the State and about
thirty years' research was . necessary in
the forming of it. Mr. Braverman states
that he has received applications from
several Eastern institutions, as well as
two on this coast for his collection, but
has concluded that the general public will
derive greater benefit from it in the Park
Museum than elsewhere. ' \u25a0
'A magnificent addition to the mineral
exhibit in the Golden Gate Park Memo
rial Museum . has been made by Max
Braverman of Visalla. It comprises a col
lection of -minerals, embraced in ten
cases, and containing many rare speci
mens. The ten cases arrived on Satur
day and are now being. unpacked and re
corded. Work will then be commenced
on a series of labels, each one of. which
will be fully descriptive of the specimen
to which it is attached.'
Some idea of the : extent of' the collec
tion may be gained; from the fact that at
least several weeks' time will be required
in -the preparation and printing of the
labels alone. : In the meanwhile the col
lection : will be temporarily installed In
flat top cases and kept together In the
center of Mineral Hall,: so that the public
may have an opportunity of seeing it in
its entirety. Later it will be arranged in
systematic order in the general mineral
collection. . K ' \ .. . â¢ . .: â¢-
Its Value Is Great to Students
of Mineralogy and
Geology. â¢ '
Presents Collection of
Minerals to Park
Museum.

NEWS OF THE OCEAN.
Captain Harry Marshall of ths Govern
ment tue Slocum is very sick at the
"Wiildeck Sanatorium. His many friends
are all awaiting anxiously for his recov
ery. In the meantime Captain Palmer,
formerly chief officer of the Slocum, is
in command.
The overdue fleet Is now reduced to the
Boanoke. now out 138 days from Nor
folk. Va., for San Francisco; 15 per cent;
the Benjamin F. Packard, now out 139
days from Norfolk, Va., for Honolulu, 15
per cent; the La Tour d'Auvergne, 160'
days from Swansea for San Francisco, 10
per cent; and the Glenbreck, 164 days
from the Tyne for Valparaiso, is still
quoted at S5 r>er cent. ,
A Chinese was found swimming in the
bav at an early hour yesterday morning.
At' first he was thought to be an escape
from the Mall dock, but later the police
decided that he had Jumped overboard
from the cannery vessel Electra. The
inongol was taken to the Harbor Hospi
tal, where he said his name was Ah Jim.
CtiJttoms inspectors are keeping a watch
on Mr Ah. as they have the impression
he has no right to land In this country.
Water Front Notes.
The bark Electra, which arrived from
the canneries ,on Sunday, dragged her
anchor yesterday and drifted down on
the British ship Principality. The ves
sels were separated by the tug Sea King,
after which it was found that the Elec
tra had her rail carried away and other
mi:aor damage to her hull, while the Brit
isher only lost a small quantity of paint.

UNITED STATES ARMY TRANSPORT MEADE AS SHE APPEARED IN A GALE WHILE CROSSING v THE PA
CIFIC ON THE YOYAGE FROM MANILA TO THIS PORT, WHICH SHE HAS JUST COMPLETED, ARRIVING
IN SPLENDID CONDITION. " . . Â«
-The Meade brought np a number of cof
fuis containing the bodies of soldiers.
One of the boxes was labeled: "The re
mains of Corporal John A. Dolan, Com
piuir C, Thirty-seventh Infantry, cap
tured and starved to death by ladrones"
(robbers). It happened in Northern Lu
zon while Dolan was out with a scout
ing party. He was captured and carried
Into the fastnesses. Quite a number of
the marauders had been killed and the
remainder of the band took its revenge
by starving Dolan to death. His com
rades were hot on the trail of the rob
bers,-however, but it was months" before
the camp was discovered and the re
mains of noor Dolan, wasted to a skele
ton, recovered. They were brought to
Msinila and then shipped on the Meade to
San Francisco. %
The Meade brought over thirty pris
oners, nearly all of whom are sen
tenced for assaults upon native women.
A colored man named Willmore is under
a life sentence for shooting a comrade.
Captain H.. A. Leonhauser, Twenty-fifth In
fantry; Captain F. W. Foster Fifth Cavalry;
Captain J. W. Heard, Third Cavalry; Captain
P. E Pearce, Thirteenth Infantry; Captain C.
E. Bennett, Artillery Corps; Captain W. R.
Das hit il, Twenty-fourth Infantry; Captain N.

A. H. Mahon and H. D. Lewis.
It was expected that the men would be
stationed at the Presidio, but the chances
are that they will be scattered all over
the coast. The other cabin passengers
o:a the^Meade were:
Major Danes is well known in this har
bor, where he served with the Third Ar
tillery several' years before being ordered
to China in" the fall of 1900.
The United States Army transport
Meade arrived from the Philippines yes
terday. During the run across she ex
perienced some very heavy weather, and
tome of the men had a bad quarter of an
haur. Everything came out well in the
end, however, and Captain Wilson
brought his vessel into p<jrt in splendid
. \u25a0

SGLDIEBS WELL BE SCATTERED.
A LARGE fleet, among which was
one overdue, made port yester
day. Among: the arrivals was the
army transport Meade, from the
Philippines; the British ships
Eracadale and Marion Frazer. from New
castle. Australia; the British ship
Chelmsford. from Hamburg, and the
overdue French bark Edouard Detaille,,
from Cardiff. , (
The Marion Frazer made the banner
run of the season, coming here from
Newcastle, N. S. W., in fifty-five days.
After crossing the equator she. secured
trade winds that brought her along at
steamship speed.
On the Bracadale; John Mannen," 68
years oX age, died from natural causes
znd was buried at sea.
The Chelmsford was 1G6 days coming
from Hamburg, and had a succession of
sales in the Atlantic, during which she
iost a number of sail. Friday and Sat
trday iast she rode out a heavy south
east gale, and the chances are that it is
the aftermath of that blow that made
things uncomfortable on the bay yester
day.
The Chelmsford was 166 days coming
from Hamburg, while the Edouard De
taille was 166 aays coming from Swansea.
On the latter vessel 10 per cent reinsur
ence was paid, however, and net a cent
on the Chelmsford. The reason probably <
was that the Detaille was coal-laden .and
the underwriters feared she had caugut
f.re.

SAN RAFAEL, Oct. 28.â Superior Judge
Angellotti this morning sustained the de
murrer of the defendants in the case of
the Shafter estate against members 'of
the Country Club. Some time ago the
estate sued the club for $4500 damages for
the destruction of a barn and other build
ings upon its property. In sustaining the
demurrer to the complaint to-day Judge
Angellotti decided that the complaint did
not show that the members - sued were
members of the club at the time of the
alleged depredations or that . the estate
owned the premises at that time.
Country Club Members Triumph.

MARYSVILLD, Oct. 28.â While hunt
ing for ducks on Feather River, twelve
miles north of Marysville, yesterday
afternoon, Henry Drew, 19 years of age
and a resident of Live Oak, was accident
ally shot in the left arm and shoulder and
died a few hours later. Drew and a com
panion had been out in a boat in search
for game. They rowed to the shore and
Drew, as he stepped out, reached for his
gun, which was lying In the boat, the
muzzle toward . him and both barrels
loaded. In drawing the gun out one of
the Jiammers caught and the weapon was
discharged. The load struck Drew in the
left arm and shoulder. He died from loss
of blood. '

Eng Go, a Chinese merchant of Walla
Walla, Wash., with his two sons, sailed
from the Orient on the steamship Olyra
pia, whli'h arrived here on Sunday. Two
days before the vessel arrived Eng Go
died'and yesterday the two sons made ap
plication for admission as minor sons of
a merchant. The law says that minor
children of resident Chinese merchants
can be admitted under the exempt class;
but. as the father of these two children
died while returning, Collector Heustis
is at a loss as to the status of the de
ceased father.
PORT TOWNSEND, Oct. 28.â A new
and novel question arose to-day in the
Custom-house over the admission of two
minor sons of a Chinese merchant. It
probably will be referred to the Treasury
Department.

SANTA ROSA, Oct. 28.-Edward S.
Brigham, the Healdsburg , young man
who was so mysteriously attacked a few
nights ago while returning. home from a
visit to his sweetheart, to-day procured
a license at the County Clerk's office here
and to-morrow, at the residence of .the
bride's parents in Dry Creek Valley, he
and Miss Emma Sparks will be married.
The Rev. Mr. Hope, pastor of the Healds
burg Baptist Church, will officiate.
Sheriff Grace has several deputies work-
Ing on the case and it is understood that
an arrest is likely to be made soon. The
bullet that struck Brigham plowed a gash
in his forehead three inches long and his
escape from death is little less than mi
raculous.

BUM DEFIES
COWARDLY RIVAL
Mrs. Despiro js being taken care of by
a number of women who have become in
terested in her case, and as> soon as she
is able to leave the hospital they intend
to see that she gets employment.
"I would rather starve in a strange -citv
than return to him," she remarked. "He
could have saved me the humiliation o*
being picked up in a starving condition
if he wanted to, but as he turned a deaf
ear to my entreaties. I intend to do like
wise to his request thai 1 return to him.
1 am contented to stay where I am until
such time as I am able to procure profit
able employment"
As soon as Captain Scymo'jr learned of
the woman's decision, he vired Despiro
that under no consideration would h'.s
wife return to him.
Detective Coleman was instructed by
Captain Seymour to show the telegram
to Mrs. Desplro, who is at present at Sc.
Luke's Hospital. After reading the. tele
gram. Mrs. Despiro emphatically in
formed the detective that she did not in
tend to return to her husband.
Friday morning she "was picked up by
a policeman, who discovered her lying on
the sidewalk, and as it was evident that
she was weak from hunger, she was sent
to the Receiving Hospital. Word was
sent to her husband iniorming him of her
pitiable plight, and yesterday he an
swered by saying that he would send a
railroad ticket to her providing she want
ed to rejoin him.
Despiro and his wife separated some
time ago, and she came to this city, ex
pecting to procure employment at one of
the theaters, but failed.
Captain Seymour received the following
dispatch yesterday afternoon from th^
husband of Mrs. Ruth Despiro, known
on the stage as Ruth Richmond, the ac
tress, who was found in a starving condi
tion Friday morning on Leavenworth
street: ,
CRIPPLE CREEK, Oct. 28. 1901.
If Mrs. Desplro will return to Cripple Creek
/will send ticket. Answer Immediately.

"The idea of celebrating as we are do-
Ing to-night originated with E. C. Stock,
one of the past masters of Franklin Lodge
of this city," said Mr. Barnes, "and I am
pleased that the suggestion made by him
has resulted in one of the largest meet
ings under the auspices of Workmen that
we have had for many years. I bid you
all welcome in the name of Workman
ship."
The California Quintet followed with
musical selections-. Grand Treasurer
Booth read an original poem and Miss
Grace 1. Davis entertained with a so
prano solo. Past Grand Master Hirsh
berg delivered an eloquent address on thi
order, referring to the work it had done
in the past, what it is doing at present
and what it expects to do in the future
for the betterment of the human family,
caring for members and protecting tho
widows, orphans and relatives of mem
bers who pass away. \u25a0 ..
His address, delivered in a forcible, con
vincing manner, was frequently inter
tupted by loud and prolonged applause.
Then followed a tenor solo by F. A. Grif
fing and funny sayings by "Billy" Brack
ett, after which a class of about 200 new
members was. marched to the front oÂ£
the hall. Each member was presented
with his certificate. A number who couM
not be present were called, but, not an
swering, their, certificates were turned
over to the grand recorder.
Floor Manager A. W. Tuggey and his
assistants then took charge and it was on
with the dance till morning.

ILIiEGAX BEGISTRATION
IN THE CAPTT_UL CITY
SACRAMENTO, Oct. 28.â Superior
Judge Hart this afternoon heard the con
fession of Thomas Hardy that he had
committed perjury and sentenced him to
one year's Imprisonment in . the' County
Jail. Hardy is one of several men arrest
ed by City Detective JVIax P. Fisher for
having illegally registered In order to
vote for Mayor next week. The records
of the County Clerk were produced, show
ing, that Hardy had registered in Sep
tember last and given his residence as the
Tremont Hotel.
Although Hardy claimed to have been
a resident of the city for ninety days prior
to registering Detective Fisher swore
that Haray had not resided here thirty
days. The officer said he first met Hardy
on September 8 and the latter told him he
had been in the city only three days.
Detective Fisher presented a signed
statement made by Hardy to the. effect
that he had never. resided in Sacramento
and had come here in the early part of
September.
Fisher claims to have found evidence* of
stuffing in seventeen cases in the First
"Ward. Chief of Police Sullivan declares
that he will vigorously prosecute all reg
ister stutters.
Halmeyer was a shoemaker. His do
mestic, happiness was shattered sometime
ago and the couple decided to forget all
conjugal bliss by separating.
Mrs. Halmeyer went to live with her
mother. 'at 18 Willow avenue, and there
the man who ended his life so dramatic
ally called frequently, to see his six chil
dren, aged from one- to ten years.
No particular attention was paid to his
visit to the house yesterday evening, but
when he informed his wife that he came
there to end his existence and made good
his threat by lifting a bottle of poison to
his mouth and swallowing the. contents,
the wife was panic stricken.
She rushed out of the house for assist
ance, leaving the children to watch the
father in his dying agonies. ' Â»
Then the Coroner's deputies were sum
moned to the Receiving . Hospital, and
relatives of the shoemaker who had
ended his life in such a tragic fashion
appeared upon the scene to express their
sorrow.
The desperate and despondent man; who
had been separated from his family, for a
few months, succumbed quickly to the
poison. The ambulance was called and
his body removed to the Receiving Hospi
tal. The doctors pronounced him dead;
"I'll drink this to show you that I love
you," eXclalmed Charles G. Halmeyer, as
he swallowed the contents of .a small bot
tle of carbolic acid in the presence of his
wife and children at 18 Willow ) avenue
last evening. . -\u25a0 , .- \u25a0\u25a0":â¢-\u25a0""' â¢

The case of M. A. Bullard of the Cali
fornia Employment Agency on Third
street, charged with obtaining money by
false pretenses from three Italian labor
ers from whom he got a dollar each for
a' job at railroad construction work at
Ukiah, was dismissed by Judge Cabanisa
yesterday. The complaining witnesses
were not in court at the calling of the
case through a misunderstanding, but ap
peared later. By that time It was too
late, as the case had been dismissed.
Bullard. Case Dismissed.
'All the employes of the laundry were
at lunch when the fire broke out. " It Is
supposed- that lint, which sometimes ac
cumulates around the. pipes, ignited and
set fire to the clothes. .The woodwork
and clothes'were highly inflammable ow
ing to the constant heat and burned like
tinder. . . \u25a0 . .
â¢ Major Hooper says his loss will approxi
mate $1000. . Water ruined a great deal of
material on the : first floor and some of
the walls and floors were torn by the
firemen's axes in their effort to locate tho
flre. â¢ \u25a0
Three firemen were overcome by smoke
â Daugherty and Conlan of engine 2 and
Freeman of the fire patrol. Freeman was
caught In the basement and was brought
out of his perilous position through a hole
chopped in the sidewalk. .
When Chief Sullivan responded to the
first alarm the smoke was bo dense that
entrance to the basement where the fire
was burning was almost impossible. The
firemen were unable for many minutes
to determine the exact location of the
fire. Whether it was creeping up between
the walls, as was indicated by the smoke,
could not be told. The Chief, in a quan
dary as to the extent of the blaze, ordered
a second alarm rung in and the firemen
set to work with a will. Sections of the
barroom floor, were chopped out and'vol
umes of water directed into, the base
ment. : -. . â¢ .
Despite the efforts of the firemen, the
smoke thickened and the building became
almost completely enveloped. The Chief
then turned in a" third alarm, and an
other relay of engines' hurried to the flre.
A circulator was then set to work. This
piece, of serviceable apparatus had been
in use -but a short time when it reached
the blaze and the danger was pronounced
past. The room in which the flre started
was walled by brick, making It difficult
for the water to reach it. It contains the
steam pipes for drying the clothes. â¢
-Burned Like Tinder. \u25a0
Police Officers l Kd j Leonard, Tom . "Walsh
and- James \u25a0 Farrel were quickly on the
scene and rendered excellent service in
the' streets,' keeping the more excited off
the flre escapes and quelling any dem
onstration that would be likely -to lead to
a panic As soon as the flre was under
control t order- was restored, people re
turned to their apartments and the trunks
and other personal belongings that had
been â¢ dragged into the corridors were
taken back to their respective places. ,
The. odor of smoke, the clang of fire
apparatus and' the shouting from the
streets aroused the guests to act.on.
Many were at', lunch in the dining-room
and- they, rushed pellmell . into the lower
corridors. \u25a0 Those ; occupying rooms came
to windows and for a time appeared panic
stricken. â¢ Some shouted to spectators be
low, but i assurances. of safety were called
back. The hotelattaches displayed great
presence of mind and as quickly as possi
ble circulated' through the building, ex
plainlng-the location 'of the flames and
telling. of. the ample time for.escape In
the' event that the building should be
doomed. .- .
. Police Avert Panic.
Fire broke out yesterday in. the drying
room of the Occidental- Hotel laundry.
Little damage was done, but for a time
intense excitement prevailed, on the
streets ' and jn the hotel. The excellent
service rendered by the Fire Department
prevented. great loss. Three alarms were
sent in by Chief Sullivan because of tha
menacing; .appearance of the blaze.
â¢â¢ It " was shortly after the noon hour
when : the fire was discovered. | ' Hundreds
of pedestrians were on their way in and
out of the business section. A. dense vol
ume of smoke breaking through the side
walk ventilators on the Bush-street side
first.- attracted attention. Within five
minutes smoke filled the building and it
appeared ' as if . the . big structure , was
doomed to destruction.
Blaze Confined to Basement
. and Order Is Soon
Restored.^ v \u25a0'

and Guests Are Ter
ror Stricken.

"The Arbiter of Morals" was the title
of tm address delivered last evening be
fore a large audience at Metropolitan
Temple by Re\-. Peter C. Yorke at an en
tertainment given in aid of. the clergy
house of St. Peter's parish, of which
Father Casey is pastor.
D. F. Keefe presided, and a pleasing
proijramme was rendered by Miss Des
mond, the Knickerbocker Quartet, Miss
Annie Rooney and William Ogilvie.
The address cf Father Yorke was a de
fence of his attitude in the recent labor
strikes in this city and of the stand
tak'?n by a morning newspaper. An at
tack, extreme in its vituperation, was
made by Father Yorke on Colonel John
P. Irish, who had disagreed with the
speaker's views during the labor troubles,
and shafts of denunciation were hurled
at the newspapers that deprecated the
reign of terror existing during the strikes.
In defending the "freedom of the
preiis," Father Yorke said that the op
position to yellow Journalism following
the assassination of President McKinley
was the work of competitors of the saf
fror-hued periodicals. He characterized
the world-wide storm of condemnation as
an "attempt to cut off the freedom of the
preflB."
Defends Attitude of Yellow Journals
and His Own Course in Hecent
Labor Troubles.

The ordinance granting a permit to M.
H. de Young to erect a seven-story build
ing, 109 feet in height, on Geary street,
near Stockton, was opposed by Branden
stein, who held that it was in'viplation of
the ordinance limiting buildings on streets
less than 100 feet wide to 100 feet in
height. Reed contended that the permit
should be granted, as it was for a worthy
improvement, and that the board had al
ready granted similar privileges and
others that violated the charter, whereas
the De Young permit -was within the
province of the board. The ordinance wa3
finally passed, with 11 votes in its favor
and 3 aeainst. â¢, ' > . '

Hearty. Two Hundred People Cele
brate Birthday of President of
the United States.
In honor of the birthday of Theodore
Roosevelt, President of the United States.
a grand banquet was given last night by
the order of * Bohemians of America."
In the neighborhood of 200 Bohemians
and their guests assembled around the
banquet tables. For the occasion the
main dining hall of the restaurant was
decked in festal attire, American flags
were artistically srranged about the walls
and a portrait of President Roosevelt oc
cupied a. place of honor. The banquet
prpved a delighful affair and will long be
remembered by those who had the pleas
ure of participating.
Er. H. B. Mehrman performed the
pleasant duties of toastmaster, and the

NEW TORK. Oct. 28.â There was no very ag
gressive bull spirit manifest from the outset
In to-day's stock market, although there was
some support for special stocks from time to
time. About midday an attempt was made to
bid up a group of specialties, but the demand
lor Etocke ; was at a minimum and. prices kept
Bagging to . a lower level. The . pressure was
not severe through the earlier part of the day,
but gained ' force and In the last hour of the
session became acute. The realizing was by
recent speculative buyers, evidently, shown by
the conspicuous -weakness of those stocks which
have been under recent manipulation for the
advance. The weakness became quite general
at the last and the majority of the active
stocks fell a point or more below Saturday.
\u25a0The fall In Northwestern was 5%; Delaware &
Hudson. 4; St. Paul. 3%; Amalgamated Copper,
3V4: General Electric and Colorado Fuel, 3;
Sugar, 2%', New York Central. 2%; Union Pa
cific. 2% ; Missouri Pacific, Northern . Pacific
preferred. Baltimore & Ohio. Tennessee Coal
and Reading preferred, 2. The taking of bear
profits by the room traders caused only feeble
rallies and the closing was weak at about the
lowest. Undoubtedly the hardening tendency
of the money market and the prospect of gold
6hlpments to Paris this week prompted the
epeeulatlve liquidation. â¢ The clouds '\u25a0 of uncer
tainty which have gathered again over the
Northern Pacific settlement â¢ seemed' -to cast
gloom over the Epeeulatlve mind after Mr. Mor
gan had arrived at his office and no instan
taneous statement of settlement was given out.
The renewed weakness of ' Amalgamated Cop
per was also an unsettling influence. .The sell
ing of this stock and of Sugar was very heavy
for Boston, account. Call , loans advanced to
day to 4 per cent and the assurance which was
professed on Saturday over the "increased cash
reserves of the banks was dissipated and yield
ed to anxiety on account of the large loan ex
pansion. Foreign- exchange continued firm to-'
New York Stock Market.

CHICAGO, Oct. 28.â Lower cables brought the
depression ' in wheat at the opening, December
starting . %c lower, at 71M>@71c. Report had
It that there had been good rains In Argentina
and at once there was a general liquidation,
not *only of large accounts, but also for short
sellers. The world's visible- supply had In
creased and world's -shipments had decreased
and there was an attempt to press the market
under 71c â¢ No sooner had the bull clique got
rid of their- wheat, however, than there came
reports that- the rain -was In ; provinces where
it was not needed. and December took a quick
upturn. It only reached 71%@71V4c,' however,
and when the pit showed signs of growing
dullness prices- eased oft again- to. 71c. De
cember closed weak, %(f^V4c lower, at 71%c.
Corn started weak because of the selling
pressure In wheat. Ibut turned up slightly as
soon as the other pit began to recover. De
cember closed easy, %c lower, 56%c. < -'-t
A fair cash demand kept the. oats pit fairly
firm and independent of ether markets. De
cember closed a shade lower, at 36%@36V4c. ;
"Hog products were ; profoundly dull, having
only enough business to establish quotations.
Prices opened lower on a *weaker hog market
and on offerings and changing of deliveries
eased a trifle. January pork closed 20c lower;
lard, 12yjc down and" ribs. 10c lower.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The pressure has risen over the northern
portion of the coast and has fallen over the
southern portion. Rain has fallen generally
over the country west of the- Rocky Mountain?,
Snow is reDorted in the Sierras moderately
heavy.
Much colder weather prevails in California
and Nevada. Freezing temperatures may bÂ»
expected in Northern Nevada Tuesday morn
ing, and east and north bound travelers are
â¢warned to exaect relatively cold weather.
High- winds are reported in Southern Utah,
Southern Nevada and along the northern
coast of California. Â«
- Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnisht. October 29. 1901:
Northern California â Clearing Tuesday; cool
er; fresh northerly winds. .
Southern Californiaâ Clearing Tuesday; cool
er in the Interior; fresh northerly winds.
Nevadaâ Clearing; colder; \u25a0 brisk northerly
winds. \u25a0 -\u25a0\u25a0 â¢
San Francisco and vicinity â Fair Tuesday;
cooler: brisk westerly wind.
ALEXANDER G. McADIE.
Forecast Official.

day, but no gold was engaged for export to
Germany by to-morrow's steamers. In fact,
the discount rate at Berlin eased off a fraction
to-day and sterling exchange hardened, indi
cating a relaxation of th'e pressure at ' that
point There was no rally, however, In sterling
exchange at Paris and it is generally expected
that gold will go from New York to that point
on Thursday in considerable volume. . Call
money stiffened Bharply In London and open
market discount rates rose above the bank
rate, indicating an early advance by the bank.
In the domestic money market renewed activ
ity of money at Western points was reported
and New York Exohange at Chicago dropped
back -to 25c discount, while shipments to New
Orleans from here continue. The optimists
urge that any considerable advance in money
rates here would divert funds to thia center.
But the analogy of the fall of 1898 and the evi
dent break of the Boer war is fresh In mind
when the call loan rate on the Stock Exchange
advanced to 186 per cent without / preventing
the current export movement of "gold. The
dally surplus of the Government revenue is also
rapidly augmenting in the latter part of the
month, making an additional drain on the
money market.

somewhat by the weakness In stocks, . but

GEORGE DIXOH" IADSES
FIGHT WITH ATTELL
I Heferee Slier Gives the Decision to
| the Former, San Francisco
ST. LOUIS, Oct. 2S.â Abe Attell of. San
Francisco and Greorge Dixon of New York,
former feather-weight champion of the
world, fought fifteen rounds before a large
audience at the West EndiClub to-night,
Referee George Slier giving the decision
to the California boy. The milling was
tame throughout, the blows of both prin
cipals lacking steam. There was a great
deal of clinching, in every round. The de
cision was displeasing to the majority of
the spectators, who were of the. opinion
that Dixon was \u25a0 entitled to a draw at
least. The boys fought at 116 pounds. '

MANCHESTER, Oct 28.â The Guardian
prints a letter, dated August 15, written
by Steyn, ex-President of â¢ the Orange
Free State, to .General Lord Kitchener,
commanding the British forces in South
Africa, discussing the military situation.
The letter] is too long to quote textually,'
but in It Steyn says In the Free State
"order and peace are' maintained by us,
and not by your Excellency. If your Ex
cellency will permit me, your Excellency's
jurisdiction extends only as far as your
Excellency's cannon can reach. If It .Is
a crime to- fight in . self-defense, and ' if
such crime must be punished, then I think
his Majesty's Government, ought to be
satisfied -with the destruction ot the coun
try, the chastisement of women and chil
dren, arid the general misery which has
been caused by this war." ...
Steyn deals with the misery of the con
centration camps. He states th@.t a great
majority of the fighting Boers are stllL
in arms, and gives it as his opinion it is
in Kitchener's power more, than that of
any other man to make an end" of the
war. He concludes- by declaring the Boers
ask for no magnanimity, but only demand
justice. .V- -\u25a0\u25a0\u25a0'.

Costs 'Twice as . Much to Marry in
California as It Does in
Ohio.
OAKLAND, Oct. 2S.-Judge Stetson, City
Justice of the Peace; Is out 50 cents. Ha
married OweivP. Mellen of Akron, Ohio,
to an Oaklana girl to-day in his ,court
ropm and when the nuptial knot had been
drawn good and tight the bridegroom
asked him how much it was.
"Three dollars!" '
"Haven't got it!" exclaimed the bride
groom. "Only got two and a half."
J'Well, that's what the code allows us
to' charge."
"Seems to me you charge an awful lot
to marry a feller out here. Back in Ohio
they only charge $1 50. Why, I could have
got a minister to do it for nothing."
Finally Judge Stetson accepted the J2 50.
As he is a salaried officer and turns in
all his fees he will have to die down,
unless the. bridegroom shows up and pro
vides enough to make. up the full $3, tee
penalty, for failure to do which is im
peachment.

Strenuous efforts are being' made to have
Stanford represented at the intercollegi

and. \u25a0 best organized force of "rooters'*
that has ever represented the cardinal.
Six hundred seats for: the game, to bo

expected that every. one will be occupied
the day of the game by a Stanford man
with a red hat and leathern lungs. Four
hundred "rooters' hats" of red cloth, have
been ordered by students trl> to the .'pres
ent date and 200 more will'be ordered be
fore the game: An effort is being made
to have'Sanford alumni in San Franciso
wear the "rooters' hats" and join with
the undergraduates in the "rooters' " sec
tion. .

LONDON, Oct. 2S.-The pertinacity with
which society discusses the rumors N re
garding the health of King Edward has
had a serious effect on tne 'court dress
makers, who expected <o have been ere
now busily engaged on the eourt dresses.
They are keeping large | staffs in almost
complete idleness. .The members of so
ciety have apparently made up their
minds that they will not go to any ex
pense until they are sure that the cor
onation will really occur. There is a very
clearly defined impression in the diplo
matic corps Uiat the King is threatened
with some organic corrfplaint.' Some go
so far as' to say it Is cancer, or Bright's
disease, cr both: Probably. the truer so
lution is that th.c cancer rumor springs
from \u25a0 the King's oft-expressed dread of
cancer, which has made such ravages in
his own family, and that whatever ail
ment may threaten him it has , ; not
reached any definite cr dangerous stage,
else he would be quite unable to keep
up" his incessant round* of public duties
and private pastimes with the regularity
and zest which - he continues to display.
In spite. of these tacts, there is undoubt
edly a very alarming premonition in court
circles, especially among women;. that the
King will never live to be crowned,
which, however .baseless, is having seri
ous eifect on trade. The favorite story
in this connection now current is thnt a
palmist told i^ueon Alexandra not long
ugo that she would never live to be
crowned.
WASHINGTON, Oct. 28.-The incoming
mail, from the East has brought to the
Navy Department a number of Chinese
newspapers which contain the news o*
what is termed an "outrage" upon an
American .citizen, andalso a British citi
zen, employed by the lumber firm of Bush
Bros, at Niuchwang. It is said that these
two men, whose names \ are not given,
were arrested illegally and threatened
with loaded rifles and bayonets by a, : party
of Cossacks outside the Russian lines at
Niuchwang on the 12th of August last, an-i
that both the British and the American
Consuls at Niuchwang have been making
vigorous representations upon the sub
ject.
The State Department has received from
Minister Conger at Peking a translation
of a series . of preliminary- regulations
adopted by the recently organized Chi
nese ..board of national administration,
charged with the reorganization of that
Government on modern and efficient lines.
The sentiments ! expressed- are conserva
tive, .says Conger, and it is made plain
that there is , no intention to imitate the
too brisk pace set by the reformers of
1898, but instead to study Western meth
ods, and, without' adopting' Western civ
ilization as a whole, to' adapt to Chinese
conditions such institutions as seem, likely
to add strength to the state. ~.:\ V \u25a0\u25a0.

for failure to run the trains at least once
In six months. However, the board was

Trustee Staats suggested looking up tha

developed the. fact that that document
contained a clause fixing a penalty of
$5000 for non-conformity to the conditions.
Staats discovered that a bond for $5000
had been put up years ago by the railroad
company as an assurance of good faith
and he* wanted the board to make an
effort to get at the subscribers to the in
strument."

Naval Cadet Noa was appointed from
Tennessee and entered the Annapolis
Academy in September, 1S96. After his
graduation in June of 1900 he went to the
Philippines in the performance of the
required two years' service abroad and
was one of the two officers aboard the
email gunbeat Mariveles which has been
doing patrol duty among the smaller
Islands of the Philippine group.
It developed to-day that Naval Cadet
Ivoveman Noa, who lost his life near
Nipa-Nipa, Samar. was killed by natives
while on a boat expedition. Rear Admiral
Rodg-ersi in his dispatch announcing the
death of Noa, gave no other casualties.
Rodgrers' cablegram follows:
"Naval Cadet Loveman Noa, while on
shore near Nipa-Nipa, Samar, on boat ex
pedition, was killed by natives October

retary Root In the face of. information

r.eed<?<3 to keep down the rebellion of "the
trhole people" in the Philippine Islands,
to Quote Major General Chaffee. The

is 7000 less than that now in the Philip
pines, and 10,000 more than the authori
ties inttaded a month ago to keep in the
islands. It is proposed to send to Ma
nila by January IS the troops required
to take the place of those which will be
brought home. General Chaffee will take
advantage of the dry season, which will
begin next month, to carry on military
operations not only in Samar. but in Lu
zon and other islands where they are
